Quiet BrainscanOpen sidebarQuiet BrainscanGPT-4This is interesting, I thought of transient hypofrontality and psychological flow as synonyms:

entered an altered state called âtransient hypofrontalityâ (Dietrich, 2003), which is associated with reductions in pain, feelings of floating, feelings of peacefulness, feelings of living in the here and now and time distortions. In contrast, entered the altered state known as âflowâ (Csikszentmihalyi, 1991), which is associated with focused attention, a loss of self-consciousness and optimal performance of a task. 1 / 1ChatGPTTransient hypofrontality and psychological flow are related concepts, but they aren't exactly the same. Here's a brief distinction between the two:Transient Hypofrontality: As described by Dietrich (2003), transient hypofrontality is a neurobiological concept. The term refers to the temporary downregulation of the brain's prefrontal cortex. This reduced activity in the prefrontal cortex can lead to sensations of reduced self-awareness, distortions in the perception of time, feelings of floating, and a decreased sense of pain. Some researchers suggest that activities like meditation, running, or even daydreaming can lead to transient hypofrontality.Flow: Introduced by Csikszentmihalyi (1991), flow refers to a psychological state where one is fully immersed in an activity, leading to heightened focus and optimal performance. During flow, there's a loss of self-consciousness and people often lose track of time. Activities that can induce flow vary for individuals but often include tasks that strike a balance between challenge and skill.While both concepts involve altered states of consciousness, the primary difference lies in their focus. Transient hypofrontality is more about a specific neurological state, while flow is a broader psychological concept concerning optimal experience and engagement.However, the two can overlap. For example, during intense physical or mental activities that induce flow, one might experience aspects of transient hypofrontality. Still, it's essential to recognize that they are distinct concepts with unique features.1 / 1Skip to content

And that

Hypofrontality

Hypofrontality is a state of decreased cerebral blood flow (CBF) in the prefrontal cortex of the brain. Hypofrontality is symptomatic of several neurological medical conditions, such as schizophrenia, attention deficit hyperactivity disorder (ADHD), bipolar disorder, and major depressive disorder. This condition was initially described by Ingvar and FranzÃ©n in 1974, through the use of xenon blood flow technique with 32 detectors to image the brains of patients with schizophrenia.

This finding was confirmed in subsequent studies using the improved spatial resolution of positron emission tomography with the fluorodeoxyglucose (18F-FDG) tracer. Subsequent neuroimaging work has shown that the decreases in prefrontal CBF are localized to the medial, lateral, and orbital portions of the prefrontal cortex. Hypofrontality is thought to contribute to the negative symptoms of schizophrenia

And that

In positive psychology, a flow state, also known colloquially as being in the zone, is the mental state in which a person performing some activity is fully immersed in a feeling of energized focus, full involvement, and enjoyment in the process of the activity. In essence, flow is characterized by the complete absorption in what one does, and a resulting transformation in one's sense of time.

https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypofrontality

https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flow_(psychology)

Psychological flow

Neurocognitive mechanisms underlying the experience of flow
Arne Dietrich 1
Affiliations expand
PMID: 15522630 DOI: 10.1016/j.concog.2004.07.002
Full text linksCite
Abstract
Recent theoretical and empirical work in cognitive science and neuroscience is brought into contact with the concept of the flow experience. After a brief exposition of brain function, the explicit-implicit distinction is applied to the effortless information processing that is so characteristic of the flow state. The explicit system is associated with the higher cognitive functions of the frontal lobe and medial temporal lobe structures and has evolved to increase cognitive flexibility. In contrast, the implicit system is associated with the skill-based knowledge supported primarily by the basal ganglia and has the advantage of being more efficient. From the analysis of this flexibility/efficiency trade-off emerges a thesis that identifies the flow state as a period during which a highly practiced skill that is represented in the implicit system's knowledge base is implemented without interference from the explicit system. It is proposed that a necessary prerequisite to the experience of flow is a state of transient hypofrontality that enables the temporary suppression of the analytical and meta-conscious capacities of the explicit system. Examining sensory-motor integration skills that seem to typify flow such as athletic performance, writing, and free-jazz improvisation, the new framework clarifies how this concept relates to creativity and opens new avenues of research.

SURPRISING SCIENCE â NOVEMBER 4, 2014
Your Brain Performs Better When It Slows Down, with Steven Kotler
Best-selling author Steven Kotler discusses hypofrontality -- literally the slowing of the brain's prefrontal cortex -- and how it allows one to enter an optimal state of consciousness, known as flow. As Kotler explains, flow refers to those moments of total absorption when we get so focused on the task at hand that everything else disappears

Share Your Brain Performs Better When It Slows Down, with Steven Kotler on Facebook
Share Your Brain Performs Better When It Slows Down, with Steven Kotler on Twitter
Share Your Brain Performs Better When It Slows Down, with Steven Kotler on LinkedIn
Best-selling author Steven Kotler recently visited Big Think to discuss the optimization of consciousness through flow states, a key topic in his recently published book, The Rise of Superman. The best way to describe a flow state is to use the example of practically every action movie released since The Matrix. Experiencing flow is similar to being in âbullet time.â Like Keanu Reevesâ Neo (though certainly not on his level), a person in flow obtains the ability to keenly hone their focus on the task at hand so that everything else disappears.

Kotler describes it like this:

âSo our sense of self, our sense of self-consciousness, they vanish. Time dilates which means sometimes it slows down. You get that freeze frame effect familiar to any of you who have seen The Matrix or been in a car crash. Sometimes it speeds up and five hours will pass by in like five minutes. And throughout all aspects of performance, mental and physical, go through the roof.â

While this may sound like sci-fi hocus pocus, Kotler explains that beneath a flow state lies âa complicated mass of neurobiology.â Thereâs a common myth that humans only use about 10% of their brains. If we were to assume this (and we wouldnât, because itâs bogus), an optimal performance would then mean the brain works harder and faster to achieve 100% efficiency. As Kotler explains, the 10% myth has it all backwards:

âIn flow, parts of the brain arenât becoming more hyperactive, theyâre actually slowing down, shutting down. The technical term for this is transient, meaning temporary, hypo frontality. Hypo â H â Y â P â O â itâs the opposite of hyper means to slow down, to shut down, to deactivate. And frontality is the prefrontal cortex, the part of your brain that houses your higher cognitive functions, your sense of morality, your sense of will, your sense of self.â

The prefrontal cortex also calculates time. When we experience transient hypofrontality, we lose the ability to assess past, present, and future. As Kotler explains it, âweâre plunged into what researchers call the deep now.â

TOP STORIES
So what causes transient hypofrontality? It was once assumed that flow states are an affliction reserved only for schizophrenics and drug addicts, but in the early 2000s a researcher named Aaron Dietrich realized that transient hypofrontality underpins every altered state â from dreaming to mindfulness to psychedelic trips and everything inbetween. Sometimes these altered states involve other parts of the brain shutting down. For example, when the dorsolateral prefrontal cortex disconnects, your sense of self-doubt and the brainâs inner critic get silenced. This results in boosted states of confidence and creativity.

Kotler describes it as his mission over the past 15 years to reclaim the study of flow states from âthe hippie communityâ and place it back within the gaze of hard science. While researchers have been studying flow for over 140 years, recent advances in brain imaging technology have led to significant neuroscientific revelations and should lead to many more. Whether or not weâll be able to develop a âNeo switchâ so that bullet time can be all the timeâ¦ well, I suppose it depends on just how far the rabbit hole goes.

Bees, like many other insects, have complex behaviors and exhibit signs of memory and learning despite their small brains. Here's how this could relate to energy expenditure and memory:Energy Expenditure & Foraging Patterns:For bees, energy is primarily derived from nectar. When a bee discovers a rich source of nectar, it returns to the hive and performs a "waggle dance" to communicate the location of the nectar source to other bees. The duration and vigor of this dance can indicate the quality of the source, which might indirectly signify the energy expended or gained.Spatial Memory:Bees exhibit impressive spatial memory, remembering landmarks and paths to and from their hive. They optimize their routes based on learned experiences. Over time, this could mean that bees develop a sense of which routes or foraging areas require more or less energy.Learning & Adaptation:Bees can learn and adapt to their environment. If a particular foraging expedition required a significant amount of energy (due to distance, adverse wind conditions, etc.) without a satisfactory reward (insufficient nectar), the bee might be less inclined to revisit that location, favoring more energy-efficient sources.Trade-off between Energy and Reward:In nature, there's often a balance between energy expenditure and the reward gained. If a bee consistently finds that a particular location requires too much energy for too little nectar, it might prioritize other locations. This decision-making process is a manifestation of the bee's memory and learning capabilities.While it's unlikely that bees have a conscious memory of "how tiring" a trip was in the way humans might remember a strenuous workout, they do seem to integrate information about the value of resources and the energy required to obtain them. Over time, these experiences shape their foraging patterns and preferences.1 / 1I think it is analogous to the expectation in scientific reasoning that explanations be parsimonious.1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely! The principle of parsimony, often referred to as Occam's Razor, suggests that given multiple explanations for an observed phenomenon, the simplest one (i.e., the one that makes the fewest assumptions) is usually the best. The idea is to favor the explanation that requires the least amount of speculative leaps.In the context of bees and their energy expenditure:Efficiency and Simplicity: Just as parsimony values simplicity in explanations, bees value efficiency in their foraging patterns. They will likely favor paths and sources that provide the most nectar for the least amount of energy. It's a simple, direct way of maximizing reward while minimizing effort.Memory and Learning Patterns: Over time, through iterative feedback, bees refine their foraging paths. They drop paths that are inefficient or no longer rewarding. This is similar to how scientists refine theories over time, discarding elements that are superfluous or unsupported by evidence.Economy of Effort: Both parsimonious scientific reasoning and bee foraging behavior emphasize an economy of effort. In science, unnecessary complexity can obfuscate the truth. In nature, unnecessary complexity can waste valuable energy and resources.Decision Making Based on Evidence: Just as scientists weigh evidence to decide which theory is most likely correct, bees weigh the evidence (in terms of nectar quantity and quality) to decide which foraging paths are worth pursuing.Drawing an analogy between the natural behaviors of organisms like bees and human-made principles like parsimony can shed light on how efficiency and simplicity are favored across different domains, from the natural world to intellectual pursuits.1 / 1I also call it Bayesian reasoning with sparse recursion.1 / 1ChatGPT"Bayesian reasoning with sparse recursion" is a fascinating way to conceptualize both the parsimony principle and bee foraging behaviors. Let's break this down:Bayesian Reasoning: This refers to a method of probability interpretation in which Bayes' theorem is used to update the probability for a hypothesis as more evidence or information becomes available. In simpler terms, it's about refining beliefs based on new data. Bees, in their foraging, are constantly updating their "beliefs" about where the best nectar sources are based on their most recent experiences and the shared experiences of other bees.Sparse Recursion: Recursion in computational terms refers to a process calling upon itself. In a broader sense, recursion can refer to iterative or repetitive processes. "Sparse" recursion would suggest an iterative process that is efficient, streamlined, or reduced in complexity. It's about going through the necessary loops but not wasting time or energy on unnecessary repetitions. When bees refine their paths, they're undergoing an iterative process of finding the best sources of nectar, but they aim to do it with the least amount of repetitive, unnecessary journeys.Drawing it all together, bees might be seen as employing a kind of "Bayesian reasoning with sparse recursion" in their behaviors. They update their understanding of the environment based on new data (like Bayesian updating) and undergo a process of refining their paths, but in the most efficient way possible (sparse recursion). This mirrors the principle of parsimony in scientific reasoning, where the simplest, most straightforward explanation is favored, much like bees favor the most direct, rewarding paths when foraging.1 / 1Help | Advanced Search

Ten Lessons We Have Learned in the New "Sparseland": A Short Handbook for Sparse Neural Network Researchers
Shiwei Liu, Zhangyang Wang
This article does not propose any novel algorithm or new hardware for sparsity. Instead, it aims to serve the "common good" for the increasingly prosperous Sparse Neural Network (SNN) research community. We attempt to summarize some most common confusions in SNNs, that one may come across in various scenarios such as paper review/rebuttal and talks - many drawn from the authors' own bittersweet experiences! We feel that doing so is meaningful and timely, since the focus of SNN research is notably shifting from traditional pruning to more diverse and profound forms of sparsity before, during, and after training. The intricate relationships between their scopes, assumptions, and approaches lead to misunderstandings, for non-experts or even experts in SNNs. In response, we summarize ten Q\&As of SNNs from many key aspects, including dense vs. sparse, unstructured sparse vs. structured sparse, pruning vs. sparse training, dense-to-sparse training vs. sparse-to-sparse training, static sparsity vs. dynamic sparsity, before-training/during-training vs. post-training sparsity, and many more. We strive to provide proper and generically applicable answers to clarify those confusions to the best extent possible. We hope our summary provides useful general knowledge for people who want to enter and engage with this exciting community; and also provides some "mind of ease" convenience for SNN researchers to explain their work in the right contexts. At the very least (and perhaps as this article's most insignificant target functionality), if you are writing/planning to write a paper or rebuttal in the field of SNNs, we hope some of our answers could help you!1 / 1ChatGPTThe article titled "Ten Lessons We Have Learned in the New 'Sparseland': A Short Handbook for Sparse Neural Network Researchers" by Shiwei Liu and Zhangyang Wang seems to be an invaluable resource for those delving into the field of Sparse Neural Networks (SNNs). It's a reflective piece that doesn't aim to introduce new methodologies but seeks to provide clarity on commonly misconstrued concepts within SNNs.Here are some key takeaways:Target Audience: This is not just for novices but also for experts in the field of SNNs. The authors are drawing from their own experiences, making it particularly beneficial for those navigating similar research terrain.Shift in Research Focus: The SNN research is undergoing an evolution. It encourages clarity, understanding, and effective communication in an evolving and complex domain.1 / 11. Background on Sparsity in Neural Networks 
Sparsity, one of the longest-standing concepts in machine learning, was introduced to 
the neural network i eld as early as in the 1980s (Mozer & Smolensky 1989; Janowsky 
1989; LeCun et al. 1990). It was picked up again for âmodernâ deep networks in the 
late 2010s, i rst under the name of Pruning, with the primary goal to reduce inference 
costs (Han et al. 2015a; Wen et al. 2016; Molchanov et al. 2016; Gale et al. 2019; Zhang 
et al. 2021, 2022). In a few years, the research interest in sparsity was signif i cantly 
revamped, owing to the proposal of Lottery Ticket Hypothesis (LTH) (Frankle & 
Carbin 2019), which revisits iterative magnitude pruning (IMP) (Han et al. 2015b) and 
discovers that the sparse subnetworks from IMP can match the full performance of the 
dense network when trained in isolation using original initializations. LTH has since been 
empowered by weight/learning rate rewinding (Frankle et al. 2020; Renda et al. 2020), 
and the existence of LTH has been verif i ed in various applications, showing the almost 
universal intrinsic sparsity in overparameterized networks (Chen et al. 2020, 2021c,b). 
A large body of work has meanwhile emerged to pursue ef i cient training as the i nancial

â¡ The article will be continually updated and we plan to invite more contributors. Stay tuned.

2Shiwei Liu, Zhangyang Wang 
and environmental costs of model training grow exponentially (Strubell et al. 2019; 
Patterson et al. 2021). Dynamic Sparse Training (DST) (Mocanu et al. 2018; Liu et al. 
2021b; Evci et al. 2020a), stands out and receives upsurging interest due to its promise 
in saving both training and inference phases. Distinguished from the conventional pre- 
training and pruning, DST starts from a randomly initialized sparse neural network and 
dynamically adjusts the sparse topology in one training run without the need for pre- 
training, while maintaining moderate training costs by, for example, keeping the same 
sparsity ratios across all varying masks (Jayakumar et al. 2020; Liu et al. 2021a). The 
key crux of DST, âsparse-to-sparseâ, should be contrasted with the more âold-fashionedâ 
training that gradually sparsif i es a dense model (Lym et al. 2019; You et al. 2020)- 
referred to as âdense-to-sparseâ in this article. One should point out the recent ideas of 
âpathwayâ (Barham et al. 2022) or âmixture of expertsâ (MoEs) essentially instantiate 
DST too (Shazeer et al. 2017; Riquelme et al. 2021; Chowdhery et al. 2022). 
As a naive special case of DST, Static Sparse Training (SST) (Mocanu et al. 
2016; Evci et al. 2019; Liu et al. 2022b; Dao et al. 2022) sticks to a i xed sparse pattern 
throughout training and the sparse pattern needs to be pre-chosen before training. This 
is tantalizing too, since to date dynamic sparse masks during training lack practical 
hardware system support. LTH could be considered as a sibling to this category: although 
LTH utilizes costly pre-training and iterative pruning to discover âwinningâ sparse masks, 
the main contribution it delivers is an âexistence proofâ of sparse and independently 
trainable subnetworks although the construction using IMP compromises the ef i ciency 
gain. As perhaps the most important subgroup of work underlying Static Sparse Training, 
Pruning at Initialization (PaI) (Lee et al. 2018; Wang et al. 2020; Tanaka et al. 2020) 
seeks to prune weights before training based on a class of gradient-based synaptic saliency 
scores so that training over the remaining sparse weights can be more ef i cient. 
While categorized as dif f erent sparse algorithms, LTH, DST, and PaI actually share 
many similar l avors and approaches, i.e., i nding sparse neural networks that can be 
independently trained to match the dense onesâ performance, hence replacing the latter. 
However, the shift of interest from conventional pruning to the emerging âsparselandâ 
(including but not limited to LTH, DST, and PaI) only occurred in the last few years, and 
the relationships among dif f erent sparse algorithms in terms of scopes, assumptions, and 
approaches are highly intricate and sometimes ambiguous, which together cause many 
misunderstandings for non-experts or even experts. Readers may refer to an excellent 
survey article (Hoef l er et al. 2021) for a more comprehensive picture.1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! Here's a condensed overview of the provided text regarding Sparsity in Neural Networks:A Brief Overview of Sparsity in Neural NetworksHistorical Context: Sparsity in neural networks isn't a recent concept. It dates back to the 1980s but gained traction again in the late 2010s with the rise of deep learning.Pruning and Modern Networks: One of the first applications in modern networks was to prune them, reducing the inference costs. This approach underwent a revival, especially with the introduction of the Lottery Ticket Hypothesis (LTH). The LTH postulates that sparse subnetworks can achieve the same performance as dense networks if trained with the original initializations.Dynamic Sparse Training (DST): DST is a recent and significant development. Unlike traditional approaches which start with dense networks and then prune them (dense-to-sparse), DST begins with a sparse model and adjusts the sparsity during the training. This method doesn't require pre-training, making it efficient. Related concepts include the "pathway" and "mixture of experts" which are in essence variations of DST.Static Sparse Training (SST): An offshoot of DST, SST retains a static sparse structure throughout the training process. While this has the advantage of being straightforward, it lacks the dynamism that DST offers. The Lottery Ticket Hypothesis can be seen as a variation of this, offering evidence of the potential of sparse subnetworks.Pruning at Initialization (PaI): This approach involves pruning the network before training begins. It bases its pruning decisions on synaptic saliency scores. The goal is to make training on the remaining sparse weights more efficient.Emergence of "Sparseland": The recent surge of interest in sparsity, covering approaches like LTH, DST, and PaI, represents a shift from traditional methods. These newer techniques all aim to find sparse networks that can match or even surpass the performance of dense ones.Existing Complexity: As the field of sparsity in neural networks grows, so does its complexity. Various sparse algorithms have emerged, and the relationships among them are intricate, leading to confusions even among experts in the field.This summary encapsulates the main points in the provided text on sparsity in neural networks, giving a brief overview of its evolution and current state.1 / 1I think it is too technical. 1 / 1ChatGPTAlright, let's simplify it further:Sparsity in Neural Networks: A Simplified OverviewWhat's Old is New: The idea of using fewer parts of neural networks (sparsity) has been around since the 1980s. This makes the network lighter without losing much accuracy.The Lottery Ticket Idea: A recent idea, called the Lottery Ticket Hypothesis, suggests that small parts of a large network can work just as well as the whole thing if trained right.Different Ways to Use Sparsity:Dynamic Sparse Training: Starts with a trimmed network and tweaks it as it learns.Static Sparse Training: Keeps the network's trimmed structure fixed throughout learning.Pruning at Start: Trims the network before it even begins learning based on importance scores.Growing Complexity: As more researchers jump into studying sparsity, there's a lot of new methods and ideas coming out. Common Confusions in SNNs: Start from Ten Q&As 
3.1. Why bother sparse neural networks, not just dense compact networks? 
(Commonly appearing in reviewer comments like: âWhy donât just use a smaller dense 
model, but rather (like an idiot) i rst start from a bigger model and then sparsifying it?â) 
It is well-known that the performance of deep neural networks scales as a power law 
w.r.t. model capacity and training data size (Hestness et al. 2017; Kaplan et al. 2020). 
However, the memory and computation required to train and deploy these large models 
also explode (GarcÃ­a-MartÃ­n et al. 2019; Patterson et al. 2021). Sparse NNs keep the same 
model architectures with those dense large models, but only activate a small fraction of 
weights. Since state-of-the-art sparse NNs can match the utility performance of their 
dense counterparts (through either pruning or DST/PaI), sparsity can potentially be a 
free lunch to make training/inference cheaper while performing the same well. 
For the sake of convenience, let us call the source (dense) deep network âlarge denseâ, 
and the SNN generated by sparsifying âlarge denseâ weights as âlarge sparseâ. In general: 
â¢ âLarge sparseâ should âª âlarge denseâ in terms of parameter counts. Meanwhile, âlarge 
sparseâ should â âlarge denseâ in terms of the utility performance such as accuracy. 
4Shiwei Liu, Zhangyang Wang 
Meanwhile, since we steadily gain performance of the target utility by scaling up model 
sizes appropriately, then if we construct another down-scaled dense model whose param- 
eter count â âlarge sparseâ, and call it âsmall denseâ, then we should expect that: 
â¢ The performance of âsmall denseâ âª âlarge denseâ, and hence âª âlarge sparseâ too. 
Hence, given the same parameter count, ideally, it is always preferable to choose âlarge 
sparseâ since it is more performant than âsmall denseâ while more ef i cient than âlarge 
denseâ. The assumed benef i ts of âlarge sparseâ models over âsmall denseâ have been widely 
observed in various SNN scenarios including neural network pruning (Li et al. 2020), 
dynamic sparse training (Evci et al. 2020a), and static sparse training (Liu et al. 2022b).1 / 1ChatGPTAlright, let's break this down:Common Confusions in SNNs: Starting with Ten Q&AsQ: Why are sparse neural networks important when we could just use smaller, dense networks?Answer:Performance Scaling: The effectiveness of deep neural networks increases with model capacity and training data size. But as these networks grow, the computational costs and memory requirements also shoot up significantly.Sparse NNs Save Resources: Sparse Neural Networks (SNNs) maintain the architecture of large, dense models but only use a fraction of the weights. State-of-the-art SNNs can perform just as well as their dense counterparts (through methods like pruning or DST/PaI), but at a fraction of the computational cost. This makes SNNs an economically viable choice for training and inference.Comparison in Performance and Efficiency:A large sparse neural network has way fewer parameters than its dense counterpart but performs nearly as well in tasks like accuracy.If you take a dense network that has roughly the same parameter count as a large sparse network (let's call it a small dense model), its performance will be significantly worse than both the large dense and the large sparse networks.Therefore, for the same number of parameters, a large sparse network is always a better choice. It performs better than a small dense model and is more efficient than a large dense one.Empirical Evidence: This preference for large sparse models over small dense ones is supported by observations in various SNN contexts, including neural network pruning, dynamic sparse training, and static sparse training.In essence, the benefits of sparse neural networks aren't just theoretical. In practice, they can deliver the same performance as larger dense networks but at a fraction of the computational cost. This makes them a more efficient and economical choice for many machine learning applications.1 / 13.2. What is the dif f erence between unstructured and structured weight pruning? What 
is the dif f erence between weight pruning and activation pruning? 
Unstructured pruning, as the most elementary pruning form, eliminates the least im- 
portant weights based on the selected criteria, regardless of where they are. Unstructured 
pruning leads to irregular sparse patterns, which typically only see limited speedups on 
commodity hardware such as GPUs. On the other hand, structured pruning involves 
the selective removal of an entire group of weights. The def i nition of âgroupâ, which 
makes those amenable to hardware speedup, could refer to weight blocks (Gray et al. 
2017; Ding et al. 2017), neurons (Jiang et al. 2018), i lters/channels (Li et al. 2016), 
attention heads (Voita et al. 2019)), or other dedicated i ne-grained sparse patterns (such 
as N:M sparsity (nvi 2020; Zhou et al. 2021), block-diagonal sparsity (Dao et al. 2022), 
or compiler-aware patterns (Ma et al. 2020)). 
Activation pruning has lots of overlap with structured weight pruning in terms of 
their outcomes: completely removing certain intermediate feature outputs. For example, 
structural pruning by neurons in MLP layers, channels/f i lters in convolutional layers, 
or heads in self-attention layers naturally yield activation pruning. However, one shall 
notice that those are still two separate streams of research and their overlap is only partial 
because: (1) on one hand, some structured weight pruning methods do not lead to any 
reduction of output dimension. For example, applying N:M sparsity to convolutional 
layers will change neither the output channel number nor the feature map size; (2) On 
the other hand, some activation pruning methods do not come from removing weight: a 
few methods sparsify activations after computing them fully (with full unpruned weights) 
(Ardakani et al. 2017; Chakrabarti & Moseley 2019; Jiang et al. 2022b), and that often 
happens when saving memory, instead of computation or latency, is the main focus.1 / 1ChatGPTLet's summarize the given information:Differences between various pruning methods in Neural Networks:1. Unstructured vs. Structured Weight Pruning:Unstructured Pruning:Removes individual weights deemed least important based on certain criteria.Results in irregular sparse patterns in the network.These patterns don't easily translate to computational speedups on standard hardware like GPUs because the sparsity is scattered.Structured Pruning:Removes entire groups of weights at once.The term 'group' can refer to:Weight blocksNeuronsFilters or channels in a CNNAttention heads in transformer modelsSpecific fine-grained patterns like N:M sparsity, block-diagonal sparsity, or patterns that are compiler-aware.Leads to more regular and predictable patterns of sparsity, which can be optimized for on hardware.2. Weight Pruning vs. Activation Pruning:Weight Pruning:Focuses on eliminating certain weights or groups of weights from the network.Activation Pruning:Focuses on removing specific intermediate feature outputs, essentially zeroing out certain activations.Has overlap with structured weight pruning because removing certain weights (like entire neurons or filters) naturally leads to eliminating corresponding activations.Important Differences:Not all structured weight pruning methods result in activation pruning. For example, N:M sparsity on convolutional layers doesn't alter the output dimensions or feature map sizes.Some activation pruning methods don't arise from weight removal. Some methods compute full activations (without weight pruning) and then sparsify the activations. This is often done when the primary objective is to save memory rather than reduce computation or latency.In essence, while weight pruning and activation pruning are related concepts, they tackle sparsity from different angles. Weight pruning focuses on the connections (weights) in the network, while activation pruning focuses on the outputs of those connections.1 / 13.3. Is structured pruning just channel pruning? Is channel pruning the only âpractical 
meaningfulâ sparse structure on hardware? 
No, structured pruning is way more than just channel pruning- it encompasses various 
more types of sparse patterns like block-wise, neuron-wise, channel/f i lter-wise, and i ne- 
grained sparsity (see above). Channel/f i lter pruning results in a sparse pattern that 
is equivalent to a small dense network, that can be straightforwardly accelerated on 
commodity hardware and platforms. Nevertheless, other forms of structured sparsity 
(e.g., block-wise, vector-wise, N:M i ne-grained) tend to deliver better accuracy at higher 
levels of sparsity since their less restricted zero/nonzero weight positions. When combined 
with the specialized hardware (nvi 2020; Toon 2020) and libraries (Gray et al. 2017), these 
types often have stronger performance-speed trade-of f s compared to channel pruning. So, 
Ten Lessons We Have Learned in the New âSparselandâ5 
the answer to which sparsity pattern is more practically meaningful really depends on 
the hardware customization and resource availability.

Find sources: "Surprise" emotion â news Â· newspapers Â· books Â· scholar Â· JSTOR (September 2007) (Learn how and when to remove this template message)
Surprise (pronunciationi) is a brief mental and physiological state, a startle response experienced by animals and humans as the result of an unexpected event. Surprise can have any valence; that is, it can be neutral/moderate, pleasant, unpleasant, positive, or negative.1 / 1ChatGPTYes, Wikipedia often uses redirects to manage similar or synonymous topics, alternate spellings, or common misspellings. Redirects help in streamlining the content and guiding users to the most comprehensive or appropriate page for a given topic. Here are a few takeaways based on what you've shared:Emojis as Redirects: Emojis or symbols that redirect to a more comprehensive page (like the smiling emoji ð redirecting to "Surprise (emotion)") make sense. It caters to the increasing trend of using emojis in search and navigation. If you're monitoring a topic, it's worth keeping such redirects in your watchlist as they ultimately point to the primary article of interest.Handling Moves & Alternate Spellings: Pages getting moved or having alternate spellings is common. You might want to utilize the "Related changes" feature in Wikipedia, which lists changes to pages linked from the current page. This can help in tracking changes that might be of interest but aren't directly in your watchlist.Watchlist Management: Considering your vast watchlist, it's essential to have a systematic approach. Given that Wikipedia is dynamic, with articles being updated, moved, or merged, it's inevitable that your watchlist will need regular maintenance. If certain topics no longer interest you or seem irrelevant, consider pruning them. This will make your watchlist more manageable and ensure you're alerted to genuinely interesting changes.Evaluate Significance: It's good to evaluate the significance of each entry in your watchlist. This ensures you're not overwhelmed with notifications and can focus on the most relevant and intriguing updates.1 / 1I think it is appropriate that the list and with the surprise emoji because it is a nod to the work of Jurgen Schmidhuber.1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely! JÃ¼rgen Schmidhuber is renowned for his contributions to deep learning and artificial intelligence. One of his most celebrated concepts is the idea of "curiosity" or the drive to reduce "surprise" in machines. Essentially, he proposed that a machine can be "curious" by trying to minimize the surprise about its future inputs. The more the machine can predict its environment, the less "surprised" it is, leading it to seek out new information to continue learning.In a metaphorical sense, ending your list with the surprise emoji is indeed a poetic nod to Schmidhuber's idea of machines (or in this case, a user on Wikipedia) seeking knowledge and trying to minimize surprise by continuously updating and expanding their horizons (or watchlists).It's a neat way to bridge the realms of human emotion and machine learning, drawing a connection between our natural curiosity and the artificial drive we imbue in machines.1 / 1What about the relation to our discussion on psychological flow and sparse representations?1 / 1ChatGPTCertainly! The connection between psychological flow, sparse representations, and the concept of minimizing surprise (as championed by Schmidhuber) can be woven together quite intriguingly:Flow and Surprise: Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi's concept of flow refers to a state where one is fully immersed in an activity, achieving a balance between the challenge of the task and their own skill level. Being in a state of flow means being deeply engaged, often feeling that time is passing quickly. A crucial element to maintain flow is the continuous introduction of new challenges that keep the person engaged without overwhelming them. In essence, the right amount of "surprise" or novelty can sustain the flow state.Sparse Representations and Learning: Sparse neural networks or representations, as we discussed earlier, try to represent data using fewer active neurons or parameters. They essentially focus on the most crucial aspects or features of data. In the context of flow, sparse representations can be analogous to focusing on the most engaging elements of a task that contribute most significantly to our learning and understanding, filtering out the "noise."Minimizing Surprise and Flow: Schmidhuber's idea of minimizing surprise for continuous learning can be seen as a machine's version of seeking flow. Just as a person in a flow state seeks the right balance of challenge and skill, a machine seeks to predict its inputs effectively (thus reducing surprise) while continuously updating its knowledge based on new, unpredictable data. Sparse representations can be seen as the machine's way of focusing on the essential elements, similar to a human in flow who zones in on the core aspects of a task.In essence, the journey of managing your Wikipedia watchlist can be seen as an endeavor to achieve a state of "flow." By curating your watchlist, you aim to maintain a balance between being informed (reducing surprise) and not being overwhelmed (maintaining sparsity). The surprise emoji ð, in this context, serves as a reminder of the ever-present balance between novelty and predictability, challenge and skill, engagement and overwhelmâa balance that both humans and machines strive to achieve.1 / 1Yeah you can turn on live watchlist it shows you a newsreel of the topics being editited, such as when people are watching a new cleopatra documentary or there is a new discovery, but it doesn't see current news with new terms, unless I happen to be watching a red link1 / 1ChatGPTThe "live watchlist" feature, which gives you a real-time newsreel of topics being edited, is fascinating. 1 / 1ChatGPTAh, the joys and challenges of open-source platforms like Wikipedia!Your intentions to update the "master/slave" terminology were undoubtedly rooted in a desire to reflect more modern and inclusive language. In recent years, there's been a push in the tech community to move away from potentially offensive terms like "master" and "slave" in favor of alternatives like "primary" and "replica" or "leader" and "follower".However, Wikipedia's community-driven nature means that changes, especially significant ones, often need consensus. Unilateral decisions can be reverted, especially if they're seen as controversial or if they deviate significantly from established conventions without discussion. It's always a good idea to bring up such changes on the article's Talk page first to gather feedback and build consensus.Don't be discouraged! Every contribution, whether big or small, adds to the collective knowledge and evolution of the platform. Just remember to navigate the waters of Wikipedia with patience and collaboration in mind.1 / 1How long was it up?
